       <tr>
        <td class="plainlist" style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">
         <div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0">
          <div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; background: transparent; text-align: left; padding-left: 5px; padding-top: 3px">
           <a href="/wiki/Astronaut" title="Astronaut">
            Astronauts
           </a>
          </div>
          <div class="NavContent hlist" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center">
           <ul>
            <li>
             <a href="/wiki/Mercury_Seven" title="Mercury Seven">
              The Mercury Seven
             </a>
            </li>
            <li>
             <a href="/wiki/List_of_Apollo_astronauts" title="List of Apollo astronauts">
              Gemini/Apollo astronauts
             </a>
            </li>
            <li>
             <a href="/wiki/NASA_Astronaut_Corps" title="NASA Astronaut Corps">
              NASA Astronaut Corps
             </a>
            </li>
           </ul>
          </div>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
         <div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
          <ul>
           <li class="nv-view">
            <a href="/wiki/Template:United_States_space_program_sidebar" title="Template:United States space program sidebar">
             <abbr title="View this template">
              v
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
           <li class="nv-talk">
            <a href="/wiki/Template_talk:United_States_space_program_sidebar" title="Template talk:United States space program sidebar">
             <abbr title="Discuss this template">
              t
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
           <li class="nv-edit">
            <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:United_States_space_program_sidebar&amp;action=edit">
             <abbr title="Edit this template">
              e
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
          </ul>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       The
       <b>
        Apollo program
       </b>
       , also known as
       <b>
        Project Apollo
       </b>
       , was the third United States
       <a href="/wiki/Human_spaceflight" title="Human spaceflight">
        human spaceflight
       </a>
       program carried out by the
       <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">
        National Aeronautics and Space Administration
       </a>
       (NASA), which accomplished landing the first humans on the
       <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">
        Moon
       </a>
       from 1969 to 1972. First conceived during
       <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Presidency of Dwight D. Eisenhower">
        Dwight D. Eisenhower's administration
       </a>
       as a three-man spacecraft to follow the one-man
       <a href="/wiki/Project_Mercury" title="Project Mercury">
        Project Mercury
       </a>
       which put the first Americans in space, Apollo was later dedicated to
       <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">
        President
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">
        John F. Kennedy
       </a>
       's national goal of "landing a man on the Moon and returning him safely to the Earth" by the end of the 1960s, which he proposed in an address to
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">
        Congress
       </a>
       on May 25, 1961.
      </p>
      <p>
       Kennedy's goal was accomplished on the
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_11" title="Apollo 11">
        Apollo 11
       </a>
       mission when astronauts
       <a href="/wiki/Neil_Armstrong" title="Neil Armstrong">
        Neil Armstrong
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Buzz_Aldrin" title="Buzz Aldrin">
        Buzz Aldrin
       </a>
       landed their
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Lunar_Module" title="Apollo Lunar Module">
        Lunar Module
       </a>
       (LM) on July 20, 1969, and walked on the lunar surface, while
       <a href="/wiki/Michael_Collins_(astronaut)" title="Michael Collins (astronaut)">
        Michael Collins
       </a>
       remained in
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_orbit" title="Lunar orbit">
        lunar orbit
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Command/Service_Module" title="Apollo Command/Service Module">
        Command/Service Module
       </a>
       (CSM), and all three landed safely on Earth on July 24. Five subsequent Apollo missions also landed
       <a href="/wiki/Astronaut" title="Astronaut">
        astronauts
       </a>
       on the Moon, the last in December 1972. In these six spaceflights,
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Apollo_astronauts#Apollo_astronauts_who_walked_on_the_Moon" title="List of Apollo astronauts">
        twelve men walked on the Moon
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aldrin_Apollo_11.jpg">
         <img alt="Astronaut Buzz Aldrin stands on the Moon" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2700" data-file-width="2700" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Aldrin_Apollo_11.jpg/220px-Aldrin_Apollo_11.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Aldrin_Apollo_11.jpg/330px-Aldrin_Apollo_11.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Aldrin_Apollo_11.jpg/440px-Aldrin_Apollo_11.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aldrin_Apollo_11.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Buzz_Aldrin" title="Buzz Aldrin">
          Buzz Aldrin
         </a>
         (pictured) walked on the
         <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">
          Moon
         </a>
         with
         <a href="/wiki/Neil_Armstrong" title="Neil Armstrong">
          Neil Armstrong
         </a>
         , on
         <a href="/wiki/Apollo_11" title="Apollo 11">
          Apollo 11
         </a>
         , July 20–21, 1969
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Apollo ran from 1961 to 1972, with the first manned flight in 1968. It achieved its goal of manned lunar landing, despite the major setback of a 1967
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_1" title="Apollo 1">
        Apollo 1
       </a>
       cabin fire that killed the entire crew during a prelaunch test. After the first landing, sufficient flight hardware remained for nine follow-on landings with a plan for extended lunar
       <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_the_Moon" title="Geology of the Moon">
        geological
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Astrophysics" title="Astrophysics">
        astrophysical
       </a>
       exploration. Budget cuts forced the cancellation of three of these. Five of the remaining six missions achieved successful landings, but the
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_13" title="Apollo 13">
        Apollo 13
       </a>
       landing was prevented by an oxygen tank explosion in transit to the Moon, which destroyed the Service Module's capability to provide electrical power, crippling the CSM's propulsion and life support systems. The crew returned to Earth safely by using the Lunar Module as a "lifeboat" for these functions. Apollo used
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn_(rocket_family)" title="Saturn (rocket family)">
        Saturn family rockets
       </a>
       as launch vehicles, which were also used for an
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Applications_Program" title="Apollo Applications Program">
        Apollo Applications Program
       </a>
       , which consisted of
       <a href="/wiki/Skylab" title="Skylab">
        Skylab
       </a>
       , a
       <a href="/wiki/Space_station" title="Space station">
        space station
       </a>
       that supported three manned missions in 1973–74, and the
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo%E2%80%93Soyuz_Test_Project" title="Apollo–Soyuz Test Project">
        Apollo–Soyuz Test Project
       </a>
       , a joint US-
       <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">
        Soviet Union
       </a>
       Earth-orbit mission in 1975.
      </p>
      <p>
       Apollo set several major
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_space_exploration_milestones,_1957%E2%80%931969" title="List of space exploration milestones, 1957–1969">
        human spaceflight milestones
       </a>
       . It stands alone in sending manned missions beyond
       <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">
        low Earth orbit
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_8" title="Apollo 8">
        Apollo 8
       </a>
       was the first manned spacecraft to orbit another celestial body, while the final
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_17" title="Apollo 17">
        Apollo 17
       </a>
       mission marked the sixth
       <a href="/wiki/Moon_landing" title="Moon landing">
        Moon landing
       </a>
       and the ninth manned mission beyond low Earth orbit. The program returned 842 pounds (382 kg) of lunar rocks and
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_soil" title="Lunar soil">
        soil
       </a>
       to Earth, greatly contributing to the understanding of the Moon's composition and geological history. The program laid the foundation for NASA's subsequent human spaceflight capability, and funded construction of its
       <a href="/wiki/Johnson_Space_Center" title="Johnson Space Center">
        Johnson Space Center
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center" title="Kennedy Space Center">
        Kennedy Space Center
       </a>
       . Apollo also spurred advances in many areas of technology incidental to rocketry and
       <a href="/wiki/Human_spaceflight" title="Human spaceflight">
        manned spaceflight
       </a>
       , including
       <a href="/wiki/Avionics" title="Avionics">
        avionics
       </a>
       , telecommunications, and computers.
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-34">

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Background">
        Background
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The Apollo program was conceived during the
       <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">
        Eisenhower
       </a>
       administration in early 1960, as a follow-up to
       <a href="/wiki/Project_Mercury" title="Project Mercury">
        Project Mercury
       </a>
       . While the Mercury
       <a href="/wiki/Space_capsule" title="Space capsule">
        capsule
       </a>
       could only support one astronaut on a limited Earth orbital mission, Apollo would carry three astronauts. Possible missions included ferrying crews to a
       <a href="/wiki/Space_station" title="Space station">
        space station
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Circumlunar_trajectory" title="Circumlunar trajectory">
        circumlunar flights
       </a>
       , and eventual manned
       <a href="/wiki/Moon_landing" title="Moon landing">
        lunar landings
       </a>
       . The program was named after the
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo" title="Apollo">
        Greek god of light, music, and the sun
       </a>
       by
       <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">
        NASA
       </a>
       manager
       <a href="/wiki/Abe_Silverstein" title="Abe Silverstein">
        Abe Silverstein
       </a>
       , who later said that "I was naming the spacecraft like I'd name my baby."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Silverstein chose the name at home one evening, early in 1960, because he felt "Apollo riding his chariot across the Sun was appropriate to the grand scale of the proposed program."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pressrelease_4-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-pressrelease-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Spacecraft_feasibility_studies">
        Spacecraft feasibility studies
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Spacecraft feasibility studies">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_spacecraft_feasibility_study" title="Apollo spacecraft feasibility study">
        Apollo spacecraft feasibility study
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In July 1960, NASA Deputy Administrator
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hugh_L._Dryden" title="Hugh L. Dryden">
        Hugh L. Dryden
       </a>
       announced the Apollo program to industry representatives at a series of
       <a href="/wiki/Space_Task_Group" title="Space Task Group">
        Space Task Group
       </a>
       conferences. Preliminary specifications were laid out for a spacecraft with a
       <i>
        mission module
       </i>
       cabin separate from the
       <i>
        command module
       </i>
       (piloting and re-entry cabin), and a
       <i>
        propulsion and equipment module
       </i>
       . On August 30, a feasibility study competition was announced, and on October 25, three study contracts were awarded to
       <a href="/wiki/Convair" title="Convair">
        General Dynamics/Convair
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/General_Electric" title="General Electric">
        General Electric
       </a>
       , and the
       <a href="/wiki/Glenn_L._Martin_Company" title="Glenn L. Martin Company">
        Glenn L. Martin Company
       </a>
       . Meanwhile, NASA performed its own in-house spacecraft design studies led by
       <a href="/wiki/Maxime_Faget" title="Maxime Faget">
        Maxime Faget
       </a>
       , to serve as a gauge to judge and monitor the three industry designs.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chariots_feasibility_5-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-chariots_feasibility-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Political_pressure_builds">
        Political pressure builds
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Political pressure builds">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Space_Race" title="Space Race">
        Space Race
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In November 1960, John F. Kennedy was elected president after a campaign that promised American superiority over the
       <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">
        Soviet Union
       </a>
       in the fields of
       <a href="/wiki/Space_exploration" title="Space exploration">
        space exploration
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Missile_defense" title="Missile defense">
        missile defense
       </a>
       . Up to the election of 1960, Kennedy had been speaking out against the "
       <a href="/wiki/Missile_gap" title="Missile gap">
        missile gap
       </a>
       " that he and many other senators felt had formed between the Soviets and themselves due to the inaction of President Eisenhower.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Beyond military power, Kennedy used aerospace technology as a symbol of national prestige, pledging to make the US not "first but, first and, first if, but first period."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Despite Kennedy's rhetoric, he did not immediately come to a decision on the status of the Apollo program once he became president. He knew little about the technical details of the space program, and was put off by the massive financial commitment required by a manned Moon landing.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       When Kennedy's newly appointed NASA Administrator
       <a href="/wiki/James_E._Webb" title="James E. Webb">
        James E. Webb
       </a>
       requested a 30 percent budget increase for his agency, Kennedy supported an acceleration of NASA's large booster program but deferred a decision on the broader issue.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       On April 12, 1961, Soviet
       <a href="/wiki/Astronaut#Russian" title="Astronaut">
        cosmonaut
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Yuri_Gagarin" title="Yuri Gagarin">
        Yuri Gagarin
       </a>
       became the first person to fly in space, reinforcing American fears about being left behind in a technological competition with the Soviet Union. At a meeting of the US House
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_House_Committee_on_Science,_Space_and_Technology" title="United States House Committee on Science, Space and Technology">
        Committee on Science and Astronautics
       </a>
       one day after Gagarin's flight, many congressmen pledged their support for a crash program aimed at ensuring that America would catch up.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Kennedy was circumspect in his response to the news, refusing to make a commitment on America's response to the Soviets.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kennedy_Giving_Historic_Speech_to_Congress_-_GPN-2000-001658.jpg">
         <img alt="President John F. Kennedy addresses a joint session of Congress, with Vice President Lyndon B. Johnson and House Speaker Sam Rayburn seated behind him" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2230" data-file-width="2940" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Kennedy_Giving_Historic_Speech_to_Congress_-_GPN-2000-001658.jpg/220px-Kennedy_Giving_Historic_Speech_to_Congress_-_GPN-2000-001658.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Kennedy_Giving_Historic_Speech_to_Congress_-_GPN-2000-001658.jpg/330px-Kennedy_Giving_Historic_Speech_to_Congress_-_GPN-2000-001658.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Kennedy_Giving_Historic_Speech_to_Congress_-_GPN-2000-001658.jpg/440px-Kennedy_Giving_Historic_Speech_to_Congress_-_GPN-2000-001658.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kennedy_Giving_Historic_Speech_to_Congress_-_GPN-2000-001658.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">
          President
         </a>
         <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">
          Kennedy
         </a>
         delivers his proposal to put a man on the Moon before a joint session of
         <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">
          Congress
         </a>
         , May 25, 1961
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       On April 20, Kennedy sent a memo to
       <a href="/wiki/Vice_President_of_the_United_States" title="Vice President of the United States">
        Vice President
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">
        Lyndon B. Johnson
       </a>
       , asking Johnson to look into the status of America's space program, and into programs that could offer NASA the opportunity to catch up.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jfkmemo_12-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-jfkmemo-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Johnson responded approximately one week later, concluding that "we are neither making maximum effort nor achieving results necessary if this country is to reach a position of leadership."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lbjmemo_14-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-lbjmemo-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       His memo concluded that a manned Moon landing was far enough in the future that it was likely the United States would achieve it first.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lbjmemo_14-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-lbjmemo-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       On May 25, 1961, twenty days after the first US manned spaceflight
       <a href="/wiki/Mercury-Redstone_3" title="Mercury-Redstone 3">
        <i>
         Freedom 7
        </i>
       </a>
       , Kennedy proposed the manned Moon landing in a
       <i>
        Special Message to the Congress on Urgent National Needs
       </i>
       :
      </p>
      <blockquote>
       <p>
        "Now it is time to take longer strides - time for a great new American enterprise - time for this nation to take a clearly leading role in space achievement, which in many ways may hold the key to our future on Earth.
        <br/>
        <br/>
        ...I believe that this nation should commit itself to achieving the goal, before this decade is out, of landing a man on the Moon and returning him safely to the Earth. No single space project in this period will be more impressive to mankind, or more important in the long-range exploration of space; and none will be so difficult or expensive to accomplish."
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Special_Message_16-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Special_Message-16">
          [16]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Special_Message_to_the_Congress_on_Urgent_National_Needs" title="s:Special Message to the Congress on Urgent National Needs">
         Full text
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikisource" title='Wikisource has information on "Special Message to the Congress on Urgent National Needs"'>
         <img alt='Wikisource has information on "Special Message to the Congress on Urgent National Needs"' data-file-height="430" data-file-width="410" height="12" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/11px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/17px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/22px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 2x" width="11"/>
        </a>
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="NASA_expansion">
        NASA expansion
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: NASA expansion">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       At the time of Kennedy's proposal, only one American had flown in space—less than a month earlier—and NASA had not yet sent an astronaut into orbit. Even some NASA employees doubted whether Kennedy's ambitious goal could be met.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       By 1963, Kennedy even came close to agreeing to a joint US-USSR Moon mission, to eliminate duplication of effort.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       With the clear goal of a manned landing replacing the more nebulous goals of space stations and circumlunar flights, NASA decided that, in order to make progress quickly, it would discard the feasibility study designs of Convair, GE, and Martin, and proceed with Faget's command / service module design. The mission module was determined to be only useful as an extra room, and therefore deemed unnecessary.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They used Faget's design as the specification for another competition for spacecraft procurement bids in October 1961. On November 28, 1961, it was announced that
       <a href="/wiki/North_American_Aviation" title="North American Aviation">
        North American Aviation
       </a>
       had won the contract, although its bid was not rated as good as Martin's. Webb, Dryden and
       <a href="/wiki/Robert_Seamans" title="Robert Seamans">
        Robert Seamans
       </a>
       chose it in preference due to North American's longer association with NASA and
       <a href="/wiki/National_Advisory_Committee_for_Aeronautics" title="National Advisory Committee for Aeronautics">
        its predecessor
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Landing men on the Moon by the end of 1969 required the most sudden burst of technological creativity, and the largest commitment of resources ($25 billion; $107 billion in 2016 dollars)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inflation-USGDP_2-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-inflation-USGDP-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ever made by any nation in peacetime. At its peak, the Apollo program employed 400,000 people and required the support of over 20,000 industrial firms and universities.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       On July 1, 1960, NASA established the
       <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Space_Flight_Center" title="Marshall Space Flight Center">
        Marshall Space Flight Center
       </a>
       (MSFC) in
       <a href="/wiki/Huntsville,_Alabama" title="Huntsville, Alabama">
        Huntsville, Alabama
       </a>
       . MSFC designed the heavy lift-class
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn_(rocket_family)" title="Saturn (rocket family)">
        Saturn launch vehicles
       </a>
       , which would be required for Apollo.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Manned_Spacecraft_Center">
        Manned Spacecraft Center
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Manned Spacecraft Center">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Johnson_Space_Center" title="Johnson Space Center">
        Johnson Space Center
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       It became clear that managing the Apollo program would exceed the capabilities of
       <a href="/wiki/Robert_R._Gilruth" title="Robert R. Gilruth">
        Robert R. Gilruth
       </a>
       's
       <a href="/wiki/Space_Task_Group" title="Space Task Group">
        Space Task Group
       </a>
       , which had been directing the nation's manned space program from NASA's
       <a href="/wiki/Langley_Research_Center" title="Langley Research Center">
        Langley Research Center
       </a>
       . So Gilruth was given authority to grow his organization into a new NASA center, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson_Space_Center" title="Lyndon B. Johnson Space Center">
        Manned Spacecraft Center
       </a>
       (MSC). A site was chosen in
       <a href="/wiki/Houston" title="Houston">
        Houston
       </a>
       , Texas, on land donated by
       <a href="/wiki/Rice_University" title="Rice University">
        Rice University
       </a>
       , and Administrator Webb announced the conversion on September 19, 1961.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TNO_12_23-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-TNO_12-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was also clear NASA would soon outgrow its practice of controlling missions from its
       <a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral_Air_Force_Station" title="Cape Canaveral Air Force Station">
        Cape Canaveral Air Force Station
       </a>
       launch facilities in Florida, so a new
       <a href="/wiki/Christopher_C._Kraft_Jr._Mission_Control_Center" title="Christopher C. Kraft Jr. Mission Control Center">
        Mission Control Center
       </a>
       would be included in the MSC.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:220px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:322px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/President_Kennedy_speech_on_the_space_effort_at_Rice_University%2C_September_12%2C_1962.ogv/321px--President_Kennedy_speech_on_the_space_effort_at_Rice_University%2C_September_12%2C_1962.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:322px;height:240px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="1067.1994666667" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="President_Kennedy_speech_on_the_space_effort_at_Rice_University,_September_12,_1962.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/d/d0/President_Kennedy_speech_on_the_space_effort_at_Rice_University%2C_September_12%2C_1962.ogv/President_Kennedy_speech_on_the_space_effort_at_Rice_University%2C_September_12%2C_1962.ogv.240p.webm" type="video/webm;           <span class="play-btn-large">
           <span class="mw-tmh-playtext">
            Play media
           </span>
          </span>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:President_Kennedy_speech_on_the_space_effort_at_Rice_University,_September_12,_1962.ogv" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         President Kennedy speaks at
         <a href="/wiki/Rice_University" title="Rice University">
          Rice University
         </a>
         , September 12, 1962 (17 min, 47 sec)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In September 1962, by which time two Project Mercury astronauts had orbited the Earth, Gilruth had moved his organization to rented space in Houston, and construction of the MSC facility was under way, Kennedy visited Rice to reiterate his challenge in
       <a href="/wiki/We_choose_to_go_to_the_Moon" title="We choose to go to the Moon">
        a famous speech
       </a>
       :
      </p>
      <blockquote>
       <p>
        "But why, some say, the Moon? Why choose this as our goal? And they may well ask, why climb the highest mountain? Why, 35 years ago, fly the Atlantic? ...
        <br/>
       </p>
       <p>
        We choose to go to the Moon. We choose to go to the Moon in this decade and do the other things, not because they are easy, but because they are hard; because that goal will serve to organize and measure the best of our energies and skills; because that challenge is one that we are willing to accept, one we are unwilling to postpone, and one we intend to win ... .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rice_Speech_25-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Rice_Speech-25">
          [25]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/We_choose_to_go_to_the_moon" title="s:We choose to go to the moon">
         Full text
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikisource" title='Wikisource has information on "We choose to go to the moon"'>
         <img alt='Wikisource has information on "We choose to go to the moon"' data-file-height="430" data-file-width="410" height="12" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/11px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/17px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/22px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 2x" width="11"/>
        </a>
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       The MSC was completed in September 1963. It was renamed by the US Congress in honor of Lyndon Johnson soon after his death in 1973.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Launch_Operations_Center">
        Launch Operations Center
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Launch Operations Center">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center" title="Kennedy Space Center">
        Kennedy Space Center
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       It also became clear that Apollo would outgrow the Canaveral launch facilities in Florida. The two newest launch complexes were already being built for the
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn_I" title="Saturn I">
        Saturn I
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn_IB" title="Saturn IB">
        IB
       </a>
       rockets at the northernmost end:
       <a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral_Air_Force_Station_Launch_Complex_34" title="Cape Canaveral Air Force Station Launch Complex 34">
        LC-34
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral_Air_Force_Station_Space_Launch_Complex_37" title="Cape Canaveral Air Force Station Space Launch Complex 37">
        LC-37
       </a>
       . But an even bigger facility would be needed for the mammoth rocket required for the manned lunar mission, so land acquisition was started in July 1961 for a Launch Operations Center (LOC) immediately north of Canaveral at
       <a href="/wiki/Merritt_Island,_Florida" title="Merritt Island, Florida">
        Merritt Island
       </a>
       . The design, development and construction of the center was conducted by
       <a href="/wiki/Kurt_H._Debus" title="Kurt H. Debus">
        Kurt H. Debus
       </a>
       , a member of Dr.
       <a href="/wiki/Wernher_von_Braun" title="Wernher von Braun">
        Wernher von Braun
       </a>
       's original
       <a href="/wiki/V-2_rocket" title="V-2 rocket">
        V-2 rocket
       </a>
       engineering team. Debus was named the LOC's first Director.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA2_27-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NASA2-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Construction began in November 1962. Upon Kennedy's death, President Johnson issued an executive order on November 29, 1963, to rename the LOC and Cape Canaveral in honor of Kennedy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The LOC included
       <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center_Launch_Complex_39" title="Kennedy Space Center Launch Complex 39">
        Launch Complex 39
       </a>
       , a
       <a href="/wiki/Launch_Control_Center" title="Launch Control Center">
        Launch Control Center
       </a>
       , and a 130 million cubic foot (3.7 million cubic meter)
       <a href="/wiki/Vehicle_Assembly_Building" title="Vehicle Assembly Building">
        Vertical Assembly Building
       </a>
       (VAB) in which the space vehicle (launch vehicle and spacecraft) would be assembled on a
       <a href="/wiki/Mobile_Launcher_Platform" title="Mobile Launcher Platform">
        Mobile Launcher Platform
       </a>
       and then moved by a
       <a href="/wiki/Crawler-transporter" title="Crawler-transporter">
        transporter
       </a>
       to one of several launch pads. Although at least three pads were planned, only two, designated A and B, were completed in October 1965. The LOC also included an
       <a href="/wiki/Operations_and_Checkout_Building" title="Operations and Checkout Building">
        Operations and Checkout Building
       </a>
       (OCB) to which
       <a href="/wiki/Project_Gemini" title="Project Gemini">
        Gemini
       </a>
       and Apollo spacecraft were initially received prior to being mated to their launch vehicles. The Apollo spacecraft could be tested in two
       <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_chamber" title="Vacuum chamber">
        vacuum chambers
       </a>
       capable of simulating atmospheric pressure at altitudes up to 250,000 feet (76 km), which is nearly a vacuum.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Organization">
        Organization
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Organization">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Administrator Webb realized that in order to keep Apollo costs under control, he had to develop greater project management skills in his organization, so he recruited Dr.
       <a href="/wiki/George_Mueller_(NASA)" title="George Mueller (NASA)">
        George E. Mueller
       </a>
       for a high management job. Mueller accepted, on the condition that he have a say in NASA reorganization necessary to effectively administer Apollo. Webb then worked with Associate Administrator (later Deputy Administrator) Seamans to reorganize the Office of Manned Space Flight (OMSF).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SecretOfApollo_31-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-SecretOfApollo-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       On July 23, 1963, Webb announced Mueller's appointment as Deputy Associate Administrator for Manned Space Flight, to replace then Associate Administrator
       <a href="/wiki/D._Brainerd_Holmes" title="D. Brainerd Holmes">
        D. Brainerd Holmes
       </a>
       on his retirement effective September 1. Under Webb's reorganization, the directors of the Manned Spacecraft Center (Gilruth)
       <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Space_Flight_Center" title="Marshall Space Flight Center">
        Marshall Space Flight Center
       </a>
       (von Braun) and the Launch Operations Center (Debus) effectively reported to Mueller.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Based on his industry experience on Air Force missile projects, Mueller realized some skilled managers could be found among high-ranking officers in the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">
        United States Air Force
       </a>
       , so he got Webb's permission to recruit General
       <a href="/wiki/Samuel_C._Phillips" title="Samuel C. Phillips">
        Samuel C. Phillips
       </a>
       , who gained a reputation for his effective management of the
       <a href="/wiki/LGM-30_Minuteman" title="LGM-30 Minuteman">
        Minuteman
       </a>
       program, as OMSF program controller. Phillips' superior officer
       <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Adolph_Schriever" title="Bernard Adolph Schriever">
        Bernard A. Schriever
       </a>
       agreed to loan Phillips to NASA, along with a staff of officers under him, on the condition that Phillips be made Apollo Program Director. Mueller agreed, and Phillips managed Apollo from January 1964, until it achieved the first manned landing in July 1969, after which he returned to Air Force duty.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Choosing_a_mission_mode">
        Choosing a mission mode
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Choosing a mission mode">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:John_C._Houbolt_-_GPN-2000-001274.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2400" data-file-width="3000" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/John_C._Houbolt_-_GPN-2000-001274.jpg/220px-John_C._Houbolt_-_GPN-2000-001274.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/John_C._Houbolt_-_GPN-2000-001274.jpg/330px-John_C._Houbolt_-_GPN-2000-001274.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/John_C._Houbolt_-_GPN-2000-001274.jpg/440px-John_C._Houbolt_-_GPN-2000-001274.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:John_C._Houbolt_-_GPN-2000-001274.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/John_Houbolt" title="John Houbolt">
          John Houbolt
         </a>
         explaining the
         <a href="/wiki/Lunar_orbit_rendezvous" title="Lunar orbit rendezvous">
          LOR
         </a>
         concept
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Moon_landing" title="Moon landing">
        Moon landing
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Once Kennedy had defined a goal, the Apollo mission planners were faced with the challenge of designing a spacecraft that could meet it while minimizing risk to human life, cost, and demands on technology and astronaut skill. Four possible mission modes were considered:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <b>
         <a href="/wiki/Direct_ascent" title="Direct ascent">
          Direct Ascent
         </a>
         :
        </b>
        The spacecraft would be launched as a unit and travel directly to the Moon and land. It would return, leaving its landing stage on the Moon. This design would have required development of the extremely powerful
        <a href="/wiki/Nova_(rocket)" title="Nova (rocket)">
         Nova
        </a>
        launch vehicle.
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         <a href="/wiki/Earth_orbit_rendezvous" title="Earth orbit rendezvous">
          Earth Orbit Rendezvous
         </a>
         (EOR):
        </b>
        Multiple rocket launches (up to 15 in some plans) would carry parts of a Direct Ascent spacecraft and propulsion units for
        <a href="/wiki/Trans-lunar_injection" title="Trans-lunar injection">
         translunar injection
        </a>
        (TLI). These would be assembled into a single spacecraft in Earth orbit.
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunar_Orbit_Rendezvous" title="Lunar Orbit Rendezvous">
          Lunar Orbit Rendezvous
         </a>
         (LOR):
        </b>
        A single
        <a href="/wiki/Saturn_V" title="Saturn V">
         Saturn V
        </a>
        could launch a spacecraft that was composed of a
        <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Command/Service_Module" title="Apollo Command/Service Module">
         mother ship
        </a>
        which would remain in orbit around the Moon, while a smaller, two-stage
        <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Lunar_Module" title="Apollo Lunar Module">
         lander
        </a>
        would carry two astronauts to the surface, return to dock with the mother ship, and then be discarded. Landing only a small part of the spacecraft on the Moon and returning an even smaller part to lunar orbit minimized the total mass to be launched from the Earth.
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Lunar Surface Rendezvous:
        </b>
        Two spacecraft would be launched in succession. The first, an automated vehicle carrying propellant for the return to Earth, would land on the Moon, to be followed some time later by the manned vehicle. Propellant would have to be transferred from the automated vehicle to the manned vehicle.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
         <a href="#cite_note-34">
          [34]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_Direct_Ascent.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="398" data-file-width="372" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Apollo_Direct_Ascent.png/220px-Apollo_Direct_Ascent.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Apollo_Direct_Ascent.png/330px-Apollo_Direct_Ascent.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/17/Apollo_Direct_Ascent.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_Direct_Ascent.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Early Apollo configuration for
         <a href="/wiki/Direct_ascent" title="Direct ascent">
          Direct Ascent
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Earth_orbit_rendezvous" title="Earth orbit rendezvous">
          Earth Orbit Rendezvous
         </a>
         , 1961
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In early 1961, direct ascent was generally the mission mode in favor at NASA. Many engineers feared that a rendezvous—let alone a docking—neither of which had been attempted even in
       <a href="/wiki/Geocentric_orbit" title="Geocentric orbit">
        Earth orbit
       </a>
       , would be extremely difficult in lunar orbit. Dissenters including
       <a href="/wiki/John_Houbolt" title="John Houbolt">
        John Houbolt
       </a>
       at Langley Research Center emphasized the important weight reductions that were offered by the LOR approach. Throughout 1960 and 1961, Houbolt campaigned for the recognition of LOR as a viable and practical option. Bypassing the NASA hierarchy, he sent a series of memos and reports on the issue to Associate Administrator Robert Seamans; while acknowledging that he spoke "somewhat as a voice in the wilderness," Houbolt pleaded that LOR should not be discounted in studies of the question.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <span id="Nicholas_E._Golovin">
       </span>
       Seamans' establishment of an ad-hoc committee headed by his special technical assistant Nicholas E. Golovin in July 1961, to recommend a
       <a href="/wiki/Launch_vehicle" title="Launch vehicle">
        launch vehicle
       </a>
       to be used in the Apollo program, represented a turning point in NASA's mission mode decision.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hansen21_36-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-hansen21-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This committee recognized that the chosen mode was an important part of the launch vehicle choice, and recommended in favor of a hybrid EOR-LOR mode. Its consideration of LOR—as well as Houbolt's ceaseless work—played an important role in publicizing the workability of the approach. In late 1961 and early 1962, members of the Manned Spacecraft Center began to come around to support LOR, including the newly hired deputy director of the Office of Manned Space Flight,
       <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Francis_Shea" title="Joseph Francis Shea">
        Joseph Shea
       </a>
       , who became a champion of LOR.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hansen24_37-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-hansen24-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The engineers at Marshall Space Flight Center (MSFC), which had much to lose from the decision, took longer to become convinced of its merits, but their conversion was announced by Wernher von Braun at a briefing on June 7, 1962.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       But even after NASA reached internal agreement, it was far from smooth sailing. Kennedy's
       <a href="/wiki/President%27s_Science_Advisory_Committee" title="President's Science Advisory Committee">
        science advisor
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Jerome_Wiesner" title="Jerome Wiesner">
        Jerome Wiesner
       </a>
       , who had expressed his opposition to manned spaceflight to Kennedy before the President took office,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and had opposed the decision to land men on the Moon, hired Golovin, who had left NASA, to chair his own "Space Vehicle Panel", ostensibly to monitor, but actually to second-guess NASA's decisions on the
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn_V" title="Saturn V">
        Saturn V
       </a>
       launch vehicle and LOR by forcing Shea, Seamans, and even Webb to defend themselves, delaying its formal announcement to the press on July 11, 1962, and forcing Webb to still hedge the decision as "tentative".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Wiesner kept up the pressure, even making the disagreement public during a two-day September visit by the President to
       <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Space_Flight_Center" title="Marshall Space Flight Center">
        Marshall Space Flight Center
       </a>
       . Wiesner blurted out "No, that's no good" in front of the press, during a presentation by von Braun. Webb jumped in and defended von Braun, until Kennedy ended the squabble by stating that the matter was "still subject to final review". Webb held firm, and issued a
       <a href="/wiki/Request_for_proposal" title="Request for proposal">
        request for proposal
       </a>
       to candidate Lunar Excursion Module (LEM) contractors. Wiesner finally relented, unwilling to settle the dispute once and for all in Kennedy's office, because of the President's involvement with the October
       <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Missile_Crisis" title="Cuban Missile Crisis">
        Cuban Missile Crisis
       </a>
       , and fear of Kennedy's support for Webb. NASA announced the selection of
       <a href="/wiki/Grumman" title="Grumman">
        Grumman
       </a>
       as the LEM contractor in November 1962.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Space historian James Hansen concludes that:
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        Without NASA's adoption of this stubbornly held minority opinion in 1962, the United States may still have reached the Moon, but almost certainly it would not have been accomplished by the end of the 1960s, President Kennedy's target date.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
         <a href="#cite_note-42">
          [42]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Winslow-Meteor_Crater-_Apollo_Test_Capsule.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Winslow-Meteor_Crater-_Apollo_Test_Capsule.jpg/220px-Winslow-Meteor_Crater-_Apollo_Test_Capsule.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Winslow-Meteor_Crater-_Apollo_Test_Capsule.jpg/330px-Winslow-Meteor_Crater-_Apollo_Test_Capsule.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Winslow-Meteor_Crater-_Apollo_Test_Capsule.jpg/440px-Winslow-Meteor_Crater-_Apollo_Test_Capsule.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Winslow-Meteor_Crater-_Apollo_Test_Capsule.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         An Apollo Test Capsule is on exhibit in the Meteor Crater Visitor Center in Winslow, Arizona.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The LOR method had the advantage of allowing the lander spacecraft to be used as a "lifeboat" in the event of a failure of the command ship. Some documents prove this theory was discussed before and after the method was chosen. A 1964 MSC study concluded, "The
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Lunar_Module" title="Apollo Lunar Module">
        LM
       </a>
       [as lifeboat] ... was finally dropped, because no single reasonable
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Command/Service_Module" title="Apollo Command/Service Module">
        CSM
       </a>
       failure could be identified that would prohibit use of the
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Command/Service_Module#Service_Propulsion_System" title="Apollo Command/Service Module">
        SPS
       </a>
       ."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Ironically, just such a failure happened on
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_13" title="Apollo 13">
        Apollo 13
       </a>
       when an oxygen tank explosion left the CSM without electrical power. The Lunar Module provided propulsion, electrical power and life support to get the crew home safely.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KSC-Apollo_13_44-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-KSC-Apollo_13-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Spacecraft">
        Spacecraft
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Spacecraft">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_(spacecraft)" title="Apollo (spacecraft)">
        Apollo (spacecraft)
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Faget's preliminary Apollo design employed a cone-shaped command module, supported by one of several service modules providing propulsion and electrical power, sized appropriately for the space station, cislunar, and lunar landing missions. Once Kennedy's Moon landing goal became official, detailed design began of a
       <i>
        Command/Service Module
       </i>
       (CSM) in which the crew would spend the entire direct-ascent mission and lift off from the lunar surface for the return trip, after being soft-landed by a larger landing propulsion module. The final choice of lunar orbit rendezvous changed the CSM's role to the translunar ferry used to transport the crew, along with a new spacecraft, the
       <i>
        Lunar Excursion Module
       </i>
       (LEM, later shortened to
       <i>
        Lunar Module
       </i>
       , LM) which would take two men to the lunar surface and return them to the CSM.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SummaryReport_45-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-SummaryReport-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Command.2FService_Module">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Command/Service_Module">
        Command/Service Module
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Command/Service Module">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Command/Service_Module" title="Apollo Command/Service Module">
        Apollo Command/Service Module
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_CSM_lunar_orbit.jpg">
         <img alt="The cone-shaped Command Module, attached to the cylindrical Service Module, orbits the Moon with a panel removed, exposing the Scientific Instrument Module" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="904" data-file-width="1140" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Apollo_CSM_lunar_orbit.jpg/220px-Apollo_CSM_lunar_orbit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Apollo_CSM_lunar_orbit.jpg/330px-Apollo_CSM_lunar_orbit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Apollo_CSM_lunar_orbit.jpg/440px-Apollo_CSM_lunar_orbit.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_CSM_lunar_orbit.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Apollo_15" title="Apollo 15">
          Apollo 15
         </a>
         <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Command/Service_Module" title="Apollo Command/Service Module">
          CSM
         </a>
         in lunar orbit
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Command/Service_Module#Command_Module_(CM)" title="Apollo Command/Service Module">
        Command Module
       </a>
       (CM) was the conical crew cabin, designed to carry three astronauts from launch to lunar orbit and back to an Earth ocean landing. It was the only component of the Apollo spacecraft to survive without major configuration changes as the program evolved from the early Apollo study designs. Its exterior was covered with an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ablative_heat_shield" title="Ablative heat shield">
        ablative heat shield
       </a>
       , and had its own
       <a href="/wiki/Reaction_control_system" title="Reaction control system">
        reaction control system
       </a>
       (RCS) engines to
       <a href="/wiki/Attitude_control" title="Attitude control">
        control its attitude
       </a>
       and steer its
       <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_entry" title="Atmospheric entry">
        atmospheric entry
       </a>
       path. Parachutes were carried to slow its descent to splashdown. The module was 11.42 feet (3.48 m) tall, 12.83 feet (3.91 m) in diameter, and weighed approximately 12,250 pounds (5,560 kg).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ABTN_LV2_46-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ABTN_LV2-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A cylindrical
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apollo_service_module" title="Apollo service module">
        Service Module
       </a>
       (SM) supported the Command Module, with a service propulsion engine and an RCS with propellants, and a fuel cell power generation system with
       <a href="/wiki/Liquid_hydrogen" title="Liquid hydrogen">
        liquid hydrogen
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Liquid_oxygen" title="Liquid oxygen">
        liquid oxygen
       </a>
       reactants. A high-gain
       <a href="/wiki/S_band" title="S band">
        S-band
       </a>
       antenna was used for long-distance communications on the lunar flights. On the extended lunar missions, an orbital scientific instrument package was carried. The Service Module was discarded just before re-entry. The module was 24.6 feet (7.5 m) long and 12.83 feet (3.91 m) in diameter. The initial lunar flight version weighed approximately 51,300 pounds (23,300 kg) fully fueled, while a later version designed to carry a lunar orbit scientific instrument package weighed just over 54,000 pounds (24,000 kg).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ABTN_LV2_46-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ABTN_LV2-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/North_American_Aviation" title="North American Aviation">
        North American Aviation
       </a>
       won the contract to build the CSM, and also the second stage of the Saturn V launch vehicle for NASA. Because the CSM design was started early before the selection of lunar orbit rendezvous, the service propulsion engine was sized to lift the CSM off the Moon, and thus was oversized to about twice the thrust required for translunar flight.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Also, there was no provision for docking with the Lunar Module. A 1964 program definition study concluded that the initial design should be continued as Block I which would be used for early testing, while Block II, the actual lunar spacecraft, would incorporate the docking equipment and take advantage of the lessons learned in Block I development.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SummaryReport_45-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-SummaryReport-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Lunar_Module">
        Lunar Module
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Lunar Module">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Lunar_Module" title="Apollo Lunar Module">
        Apollo Lunar Module
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo16LM.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1232" data-file-width="1367" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Apollo16LM.jpg/220px-Apollo16LM.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Apollo16LM.jpg/330px-Apollo16LM.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Apollo16LM.jpg/440px-Apollo16LM.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo16LM.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Apollo_16" title="Apollo 16">
          Apollo 16
         </a>
         <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Lunar_Module" title="Apollo Lunar Module">
          LM
         </a>
         on the Moon
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Lunar Module (LM) was designed to descend from lunar orbit to land two astronauts on the Moon and take them back to orbit to rendezvous with the Command Module. Not designed to fly through the Earth's atmosphere or return to Earth, its fuselage was designed totally without aerodynamic considerations, and was of an extremely lightweight construction. It consisted of separate descent and ascent stages, each with its own engine. The descent stage contained storage for the descent propellant, surface stay consumables, and surface exploration equipment. The ascent stage contained the crew cabin, ascent propellant, and a reaction control system. The initial LM model weighed approximately 33,300 pounds (15,100 kg), and allowed surface stays up to around 34 hours. An Extended Lunar Module weighed over 36,200 pounds (16,400 kg), and allowed surface stays of over 3 days.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ABTN_LV2_46-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-ABTN_LV2-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The contract for design and construction of the Lunar Module was awarded to
       <a href="/wiki/Grumman" title="Grumman">
        Grumman Aircraft Engineering Corporation
       </a>
       , and the project was overseen by
       <a href="/wiki/Thomas_J._Kelly_(aerospace_engineer)" title="Thomas J. Kelly (aerospace engineer)">
        Thomas J. Kelly
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Launch_vehicles">
        Launch vehicles
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Launch vehicles">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturnsandlittlejoe2.gif">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="433" data-file-width="628" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Saturnsandlittlejoe2.gif/300px-Saturnsandlittlejoe2.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Saturnsandlittlejoe2.gif/450px-Saturnsandlittlejoe2.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Saturnsandlittlejoe2.gif/600px-Saturnsandlittlejoe2.gif 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saturnsandlittlejoe2.gif" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Four Apollo rocket assemblies, drawn to scale:
         <a href="/wiki/Little_Joe_II" title="Little Joe II">
          Little Joe II
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Saturn_I" title="Saturn I">
          Saturn I
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Saturn_IB" title="Saturn IB">
          Saturn IB
         </a>
         , and
         <a href="/wiki/Saturn_V" title="Saturn V">
          Saturn V
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Before the Apollo program began, Wernher von Braun and his team of rocket engineers had started work on plans for very large launch vehicles, the
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn_(rocket_family)" title="Saturn (rocket family)">
        Saturn series
       </a>
       , and the even larger
       <a href="/wiki/Nova_(rocket)" title="Nova (rocket)">
        Nova
       </a>
       series. In the midst of these plans, von Braun was transferred from the Army to NASA, and made Director of the Marshall Space Flight Center. The initial direct ascent plan to send the three-man Apollo Command/Service Module directly to the lunar surface, on top of a large descent rocket stage, would require a Nova-class launcher, with a lunar payload capability of over 180,000 pounds (82,000 kg).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The June 11, 1962, decision to use lunar orbit rendezvous enabled the Saturn V to replace the Nova, and the MSFC proceeded to develop the
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn_(rocket_family)" title="Saturn (rocket family)">
        Saturn rocket family
       </a>
       for Apollo.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Little_Joe_II">
        Little Joe II
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Little Joe II">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Little_Joe_II" title="Little Joe II">
        Little Joe II
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Since Apollo, like Mercury, would require a
       <a href="/wiki/Launch_escape_system" title="Launch escape system">
        launch escape system
       </a>
       (LES) in case of a launch failure, a relatively small rocket was required for qualification flight testing of this system. A size bigger than the NAA
       <a href="/wiki/Little_Joe_(rocket)" title="Little Joe (rocket)">
        Little Joe
       </a>
       would be required, so the
       <a href="/wiki/Little_Joe_II" title="Little Joe II">
        Little Joe II
       </a>
       was built by
       <a href="/wiki/General_Dynamics" title="General Dynamics">
        General Dynamics
       </a>
       /
       <a href="/wiki/Convair" title="Convair">
        Convair
       </a>
       . After an August 1963
       <a href="/wiki/Little_Joe_II_Qualification_Test_Vehicle" title="Little Joe II Qualification Test Vehicle">
        qualification test flight
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
        <a href="#cite_note-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       four LES test flights (
       <a href="/wiki/A-001" title="A-001">
        A-001
       </a>
       through
       <a href="/wiki/A-004" title="A-004">
        004
       </a>
       ) were made at the
       <a href="/wiki/White_Sands_Missile_Range" title="White Sands Missile Range">
        White Sands Missile Range
       </a>
       between May 1964 and January 1966.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Saturn_I">
        Saturn I
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Saturn I">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn_I" title="Saturn I">
        Saturn I
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_7_launch2.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2411" data-file-width="2000" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Apollo_7_launch2.jpg/150px-Apollo_7_launch2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Apollo_7_launch2.jpg/225px-Apollo_7_launch2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Apollo_7_launch2.jpg/300px-Apollo_7_launch2.jpg 2x" width="150"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_7_launch2.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A Saturn IB rocket launches
         <a href="/wiki/Apollo_7" title="Apollo 7">
          Apollo 7
         </a>
         , 1968
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Since Apollo, like Mercury, used more than one launch vehicle for space missions, NASA used spacecraft-launch vehicle combination series numbers: AS-10x for Saturn I, AS-20x for Saturn IB, and AS-50x for Saturn V (compare
       <a href="/wiki/Mercury-Redstone_3" title="Mercury-Redstone 3">
        Mercury-Redstone 3
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Mercury-Atlas_6" title="Mercury-Atlas 6">
        Mercury-Atlas 6
       </a>
       ) to designate and plan all missions, rather than numbering them sequentially as in
       <a href="/wiki/Project_Gemini" title="Project Gemini">
        Project Gemini
       </a>
       . This was changed by the time manned flights began.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-missionNumbers_53-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-missionNumbers-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Saturn I, the first US heavy lift launch vehicle, was initially planned to launch partially equipped CSMs in low Earth orbit tests. The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S-I" title="S-I">
        S-I
       </a>
       first stage burned
       <a href="/wiki/RP-1" title="RP-1">
        RP-1
       </a>
       with liquid oxygen (LOX) oxidizer in eight clustered
       <a href="/wiki/Rocketdyne_H-1" title="Rocketdyne H-1">
        Rocketdyne H-1
       </a>
       engines, to produce 1,500,000 pounds-force (6,670 kN) of thrust. The
       <a href="/wiki/S-IV" title="S-IV">
        S-IV
       </a>
       second stage used six liquid hydrogen-fueled
       <a href="/wiki/Pratt_%26_Whitney_Rocketdyne" title="Pratt &amp; Whitney Rocketdyne">
        Pratt &amp; Whitney
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RL-10" title="RL-10">
        RL-10
       </a>
       engines with 90,000 pounds-force (400 kN) of thrust. A planned
       <a href="/wiki/Centaur_(rocket_stage)" title="Centaur (rocket stage)">
        Centaur
       </a>
       (S-V) third stage with two RL-10 engines never flew on Saturn I.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The first four Saturn I test flights were launched from LC-34, with only live first stages, carrying dummy upper stages filled with water. The first flight with a live S-IV was launched from LC-37. This was followed by five launches of
       <a href="/wiki/Boilerplate_(spaceflight)" title="Boilerplate (spaceflight)">
        boilerplate
       </a>
       CSMs (designated
       <a href="/wiki/AS-101_(spacecraft)" title="AS-101 (spacecraft)">
        AS-101
       </a>
       through
       <a href="/wiki/AS-105_(spacecraft)" title="AS-105 (spacecraft)">
        AS-105
       </a>
       ) into orbit in 1964 and 1965. The last three of these further supported the Apollo program by also carrying
       <a href="/wiki/Pegasus_(satellite)" title="Pegasus (satellite)">
        Pegasus
       </a>
       satellites, which verified the safety of the translunar environment by measuring the frequency and severity of
       <a href="/wiki/Micrometeorite" title="Micrometeorite">
        micrometeorite
       </a>
       impacts.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
        <a href="#cite_note-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In September 1962, NASA planned to launch four manned CSM flights on the Saturn I from late 1965 through 1966, concurrent with Project Gemini. The 22,500-pound (10,200 kg) payload capacity
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       would have severely limited the systems which could be included, so the decision was made in October 1963 to use the uprated
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn_IB" title="Saturn IB">
        Saturn IB
       </a>
       for all manned Earth orbital flights.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Saturn_IB">
        Saturn IB
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Saturn IB">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn_IB" title="Saturn IB">
        Saturn IB
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Saturn IB was an upgraded version of the Saturn I. The
       <a href="/wiki/S-IB" title="S-IB">
        S-IB
       </a>
       first stage increased the thrust to 1,600,000 pounds-force (7,120 kN) by uprating the H-1 engine. The second stage replaced the S-IV with the
       <a href="/wiki/S-IVB" title="S-IVB">
        S-IVB-200
       </a>
       , powered by a single
       <a href="/wiki/Rocketdyne_J-2" title="Rocketdyne J-2">
        J-2
       </a>
       engine burning liquid hydrogen fuel with LOX, to produce 200,000
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pound-force" title="Pound-force">
        pounds-force
       </a>
       (890
       <a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">
        kN
       </a>
       ) of thrust.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
