       <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Management_and_Budget" title="Office of Management and Budget">
        Office of Management and Budget
       </a>
       Deputy Director
       <a href="/wiki/Caspar_Weinberger" title="Caspar Weinberger">
        Caspar Weinberger
       </a>
       was opposed to this, and persuaded Nixon to keep the remaining missions.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111">
        <a href="#cite_note-111">
         [111]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Extended_missions">
        Extended missions
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Extended missions">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_15_Lunar_Rover_and_Irwin.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1159" data-file-width="1546" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Apollo_15_Lunar_Rover_and_Irwin.jpg/220px-Apollo_15_Lunar_Rover_and_Irwin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Apollo_15_Lunar_Rover_and_Irwin.jpg/330px-Apollo_15_Lunar_Rover_and_Irwin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Apollo_15_Lunar_Rover_and_Irwin.jpg/440px-Apollo_15_Lunar_Rover_and_Irwin.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_15_Lunar_Rover_and_Irwin.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Roving_Vehicle" title="Lunar Roving Vehicle">
          Lunar Roving Vehicle
         </a>
         used on Apollos 15–17
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo17_plaque.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2356" data-file-width="2898" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Apollo17_plaque.jpg/220px-Apollo17_plaque.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Apollo17_plaque.jpg/330px-Apollo17_plaque.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Apollo17_plaque.jpg/440px-Apollo17_plaque.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo17_plaque.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Plaque left on the Moon by
         <a href="/wiki/Apollo_17" title="Apollo 17">
          Apollo 17
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Apollo 15 was launched on July 26, 1971, with David Scott,
       <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Worden" title="Alfred Worden">
        Alfred Worden
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/James_Irwin" title="James Irwin">
        James Irwin
       </a>
       . Scott and Irwin landed on July 30 near
       <a href="/wiki/Mons_Hadley" title="Mons Hadley">
        Hadley Rille
       </a>
       , and spent just under two days, 19 hours on the surface. In over 18 hours of EVA, they collected about 77 kilograms (170 lb) of lunar material.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112">
        <a href="#cite_note-112">
         [112]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Apollo 16 landed in the
       <a href="/wiki/Descartes_Highlands" title="Descartes Highlands">
        Descartes Highlands
       </a>
       on April 20, 1972. The crew was commanded by John Young, with
       <a href="/wiki/Ken_Mattingly" title="Ken Mattingly">
        Ken Mattingly
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Charles_Duke" title="Charles Duke">
        Charles Duke
       </a>
       . Young and Duke spent just under three days on the surface, with a total of over 20 hours EVA.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113">
        <a href="#cite_note-113">
         [113]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Apollo 17 was the last of the Apollo program, landing in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taurus-Littrow" title="Taurus-Littrow">
        Taurus-Littrow
       </a>
       region in December 1972. Eugene Cernan commanded
       <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Evans_(astronaut)" title="Ronald Evans (astronaut)">
        Ronald E. Evans
       </a>
       and NASA's first scientist-astronaut, geologist Dr.
       <a href="/wiki/Harrison_Schmitt" title="Harrison Schmitt">
        Harrison H. Schmitt
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apollo17_114-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-apollo17-114">
         [114]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Schmitt was originally scheduled for Apollo 18,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115">
        <a href="#cite_note-115">
         [115]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but the lunar geological community lobbied for his inclusion on the final lunar landing.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116">
        <a href="#cite_note-116">
         [116]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Cernan and Schmitt stayed on the surface for just over three days and spent just over 23 hours of total EVA.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apollo17_114-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-apollo17-114">
         [114]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div style="clear:both;">
      </div>

        <td>

          James Lovell
         </a>
         <br/>
         <a href="/wiki/Jack_Swigert" title="Jack Swigert">
          Jack Swigert
         </a>
         <br/>
         <a href="/wiki/Fred_Haise" title="Fred Haise">
          Fred Haise
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         Third landing attempt aborted near the Moon, due to SM failure. Crew used LM as "life boat" to return to Earth.
        </td>
       </tr>

         <i>

         <br/>

        </td>

       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Orloff-EVA_117-0">

         [117]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Samples_returned">
        Samples returned
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Samples returned">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Moon_rock" title="Moon rock">
        Moon rock
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;max-width:292px">
        <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:164px;max-width:164px">
         <div class="thumbimage" style="height:162px;overflow:hidden">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_15_Genesis_Rock.jpg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="1024" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Apollo_15_Genesis_Rock.jpg/162px-Apollo_15_Genesis_Rock.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Apollo_15_Genesis_Rock.jpg/243px-Apollo_15_Genesis_Rock.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Apollo_15_Genesis_Rock.jpg/324px-Apollo_15_Genesis_Rock.jpg 2x" width="162"/>
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
          The most famous of the Moon rocks recovered, the
          <a href="/wiki/Genesis_Rock" title="Genesis Rock">
           Genesis Rock
          </a>
          , returned from Apollo 15.
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:124px;max-width:124px">
         <div class="thumbimage" style="height:162px;overflow:hidden">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lunar_Ferroan_Anorthosite_(60025).jpg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1200" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Lunar_Ferroan_Anorthosite_%2860025%29.jpg/122px-Lunar_Ferroan_Anorthosite_%2860025%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Lunar_Ferroan_Anorthosite_%2860025%29.jpg/183px-Lunar_Ferroan_Anorthosite_%2860025%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Lunar_Ferroan_Anorthosite_%2860025%29.jpg/244px-Lunar_Ferroan_Anorthosite_%2860025%29.jpg 2x" width="122"/>
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
          Ferroan
          <a href="/wiki/Anorthosite" title="Anorthosite">
           Anorthosite
          </a>
          Moon rock, returned from Apollo 16.
         </div>
        </div>
        <div style="clear:left">
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Apollo program returned over 382 kg (842 lb) of lunar rocks and
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_soil" title="Lunar soil">
        soil
       </a>
       to the
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Receiving_Laboratory" title="Lunar Receiving Laboratory">
        Lunar Receiving Laboratory
       </a>
       in Houston.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118">
        <a href="#cite_note-118">
         [118]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Orloff-EVA_117-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Orloff-EVA-117">
         [117]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119">
        <a href="#cite_note-119">
         [119]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Today, 75% of the samples are stored at the
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Sample_Laboratory_Facility" title="Lunar Sample Laboratory Facility">
        Lunar Sample Laboratory Facility
       </a>
       built in 1979.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120">
        <a href="#cite_note-120">
         [120]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The rocks collected from the Moon are extremely old compared to rocks found on Earth, as measured by
       <a href="/wiki/Radiometric_dating" title="Radiometric dating">
        radiometric dating
       </a>
       techniques. They range in age from about 3.2 billion years for the
       <a href="/wiki/Basalt" title="Basalt">
        basaltic
       </a>
       samples derived from the
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_mare" title="Lunar mare">
        lunar maria
       </a>
       , to about 4.6 billion years for samples derived from the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunar_highlands" title="Lunar highlands">
        highlands
       </a>
       crust.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121">
        <a href="#cite_note-121">
         [121]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As such, they represent samples from a very early period in the development of the
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">
        Solar System
       </a>
       , that are largely absent on Earth. One important rock found during the Apollo Program is dubbed the
       <a href="/wiki/Genesis_Rock" title="Genesis Rock">
        Genesis Rock
       </a>
       , retrieved by astronauts David Scott and James Irwin during the Apollo 15 mission.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarland2008132-133_122-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarland2008132-133-122">
         [122]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This
       <a href="/wiki/Anorthosite" title="Anorthosite">
        anorthosite
       </a>
       rock is composed almost exclusively of the calcium-rich feldspar mineral
       <a href="/wiki/Anorthite" title="Anorthite">
        anorthite
       </a>
       , and is believed to be representative of the highland crust.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarland2008171_123-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarland2008171-123">
         [123]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A geochemical component called
       <a href="/wiki/KREEP" title="KREEP">
        KREEP
       </a>
       was discovered by Apollo 12, which has no known terrestrial counterpart.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarland200849-50_124-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarland200849-50-124">
         [124]
        </a>
       </sup>
       KREEP and the anorthositic samples have been used to infer that the outer portion of the Moon was once completely molten (see
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_magma_ocean" title="Lunar magma ocean">
        lunar magma ocean
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarland2008323-327_125-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarland2008323-327-125">
         [125]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Almost all the rocks show evidence of impact process effects. Many samples appear to be pitted with
       <a href="/wiki/Micrometeoroid" title="Micrometeoroid">
        micrometeoroid
       </a>
       impact craters, which is never seen on Earth rocks, due to the thick atmosphere. Many show signs of being subjected to high pressure shock waves that are generated during impact events. Some of the returned samples are of
       <i>
        impact melt
       </i>
       (materials melted near an impact crater.) All samples returned from the Moon are highly
       <a href="/wiki/Breccia" title="Breccia">
        brecciated
       </a>
       as a result of being subjected to multiple impact events.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarland2008330-332_126-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarland2008330-332-126">
         [126]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Analysis of composition of the lunar samples supports the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Giant_impact_hypothesis" title="Giant impact hypothesis">
        giant impact hypothesis
       </a>
       , that the Moon was created through impact of a large astronomical body with the Earth.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127">
        <a href="#cite_note-127">
         [127]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Costs">
        Costs
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Costs">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       When President Kennedy first chartered the Moon landing program, a preliminary cost estimate of $7 billion was generated, but this proved an extremely unrealistic guess of what could not possibly be determined precisely, and James Webb used his judgment as administrator to change the estimate to $20 billion before giving it to Vice President Johnson.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Butts_128-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Butts-128">
         [128]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       When Kennedy made his 1962 speech at Rice University, the annual
       <a href="/wiki/Budget_of_NASA" title="Budget of NASA">
        space budget
       </a>
       was $5.4 billion, and he described this cost as 40 cents per person per week, "somewhat less than we pay for cigarettes and cigars every year", but that the Moon program would soon raise this to "more than 50 cents a week for every man, woman and child in the United States".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rice_Speech_25-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Rice_Speech-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>

      </table>
      <p>
       Webb's estimate shocked many at the time (including the President) but ultimately proved to be reasonably accurate. In January 1969, NASA prepared an itemized estimate of the run-out cost of the Apollo program. The total came to $23.9 billion, itemized as follows:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130">
        <a href="#cite_note-130">
         [130]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>
         Aircraft/Operation
        </th>
        <th>
         Cost ($)
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Apollo spacecraft
        </td>
        <td align="right">
         7,945.0 million
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Saturn I launch vehicles
        </td>
        <td align="right">
         767.1 million
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Saturn IB launch vehicles
        </td>
        <td align="right">
         1,131.2 million
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Saturn V launch vehicles
        </td>
        <td align="right">
         6,871.1 million
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Launch vehicle engine development
        </td>
        <td align="right">
         854.2 million
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Mission support
        </td>
        <td align="right">
         1,432.3 million
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>

        </td>

        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>

        </td>

        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Operation of installations
        </td>
        <td align="right">
         2,420.6 million
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       The final cost of Apollo was reported to Congress as $25.4 billion in 1973,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_1-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It took up the majority of NASA's budget while it was being developed. For example, in 1966 it accounted for about 60 percent of NASA's total $5.2 billion budget.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131">
        <a href="#cite_note-131">
         [131]
        </a>
       </sup>
       That was one of the biggest investment of the US in science, research and development, and employed thousands of American scientists. A single Saturn V launch in 1969 cost up to $375 million, compared to the
       <a href="/wiki/National_Science_Foundation" title="National Science Foundation">
        National Science Foundation
       </a>
       's fiscal year 1970 budget of $440 million.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132">
        <a href="#cite_note-132">
         [132]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2009, NASA held a symposium on project costs which presented an estimate of the Apollo program costs in 2005 dollars as roughly $170 billion ($206 billion in 2016 dollars
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inflation-USGDP_2-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-inflation-USGDP-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ). This included all
       <a href="/wiki/Research_and_development" title="Research and development">
        research and development
       </a>
       costs; the procurement of 15 Saturn V rockets, 16 Command/Service Modules, 12 Lunar Modules, plus program support and management costs; construction expenses for facilities and their upgrading, and costs for flight operations. This was based on a
       <a href="/wiki/Congressional_Budget_Office" title="Congressional Budget Office">
        Congressional Budget Office
       </a>
       report,
       <i>
        A Budgetary Analysis of NASA's New Vision for Space
       </i>
       , September 2004.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Butts_128-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Butts-128">
         [128]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        The Space Review
       </i>
       estimated in 2010 the cost of Apollo from 1959 to 1973 as $20.4 billion, or $109 billion in 2010 dollars. ($120 billion in 2016 dollars
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inflation-USGDP_2-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-inflation-USGDP-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       )
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lafleur20100308_133-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-lafleur20100308-133">
         [133]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Apollo_Applications_Program">
        Apollo Applications Program
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Apollo Applications Program">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Applications_Program" title="Apollo Applications Program">
        Apollo Applications Program
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Looking beyond the manned lunar landings, NASA investigated several post-lunar applications for Apollo hardware. The Apollo Extension Series (
       <i>
        Apollo X
       </i>
       ,) proposed up to 30 flights to Earth orbit, using the space in the
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_(spacecraft)#Spacecraft_Lunar_Module_Adapter_(SLA)" title="Apollo (spacecraft)">
        Spacecraft Lunar Module Adapter
       </a>
       (SLA) to house a small orbital laboratory (workshop). Astronauts would continue to use the CSM as a ferry to the station. This study was followed by design of a larger orbital workshop to be built in orbit from an empty S-IVB Saturn upper stage, and grew into the Apollo Applications Program (AAP). The workshop was to be supplemented by the
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Telescope_Mount" title="Apollo Telescope Mount">
        Apollo Telescope Mount
       </a>
       , which could be attached to the ascent stage of the lunar module via a rack.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134">
        <a href="#cite_note-134">
         [134]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The most ambitious plan called for using an empty S-IVB as an interplanetary spacecraft for a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manned_Venus_Flyby" title="Manned Venus Flyby">
        Venus fly-by mission
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135">
        <a href="#cite_note-135">
         [135]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The S-IVB orbital workshop was the only one of these plans to make it off the drawing board. Dubbed
       <a href="/wiki/Skylab" title="Skylab">
        Skylab
       </a>
       , it was constructed complete on the ground rather than in space, and launched in 1973 using the two lower stages of a Saturn V. It was equipped with an Apollo Telescope Mount. Skylab's last crew departed the station on February 8, 1974, and the station itself re-entered the atmosphere in 1979.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136">
        <a href="#cite_note-136">
         [136]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Legacy_137-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Legacy-137">
         [137]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apollo-Soyuz_Test_Project" title="Apollo-Soyuz Test Project">
        Apollo-Soyuz Test Project
       </a>
       also used Apollo hardware for the first joint nation space flight, paving the way for future cooperation with other nations in the
       <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">
        Space Shuttle
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">
        International Space Station
       </a>
       programs.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Legacy_137-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Legacy-137">
         [137]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138">
        <a href="#cite_note-138">
         [138]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_observations">
        Recent observations
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Recent observations">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo11-LRO-March2012.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="580" data-file-width="580" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Apollo11-LRO-March2012.jpg/220px-Apollo11-LRO-March2012.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Apollo11-LRO-March2012.jpg/330px-Apollo11-LRO-March2012.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Apollo11-LRO-March2012.jpg/440px-Apollo11-LRO-March2012.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo11-LRO-March2012.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Tranquility_Base" title="Tranquility Base">
          Tranquility Base
         </a>
         , imaged in March 2012 by the Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In September 2007, the
       <a href="/wiki/X_Prize_Foundation" title="X Prize Foundation">
        X PRIZE Foundation
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">
        Google
       </a>
       announced the
       <a href="/wiki/Google_Lunar_X_Prize" title="Google Lunar X Prize">
        Google Lunar X Prize
       </a>
       , to be awarded for a robotic lunar landing mission which transmits close-up images of the Apollo Lunar Modules and
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_artificial_objects_on_the_Moon" title="List of artificial objects on the Moon">
        other artificial objects on the surface
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139">
        <a href="#cite_note-139">
         [139]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2008,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japan_Aerospace_Exploration_Agency" title="Japan Aerospace Exploration Agency">
        Japan Aerospace Exploration Agency
       </a>
       's
       <a href="/wiki/SELENE" title="SELENE">
        SELENE
       </a>
       probe observed evidence of the halo surrounding the Apollo 15 Lunar Module blast crater while orbiting above the lunar surface.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140">
        <a href="#cite_note-140">
         [140]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2009, NASA's
       <a href="/wiki/Robotic_spacecraft" title="Robotic spacecraft">
        robotic
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Reconnaissance_Orbiter" title="Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter">
        Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter
       </a>
       , while orbiting 50 kilometers (31 mi) above the Moon, began photographing the remnants of the Apollo program left on the lunar surface, and photographed each site where manned Apollo flights landed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141">
        <a href="#cite_note-141">
         [141]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142">
        <a href="#cite_note-142">
         [142]
        </a>
       </sup>
       All of the U. S. flags left on the Moon during the Apollo missions were found to still be standing, with the exception of the one left during the Apollo 11 mission, which was blown over during that mission's lift-off from the lunar surface and return to the mission Command Module in lunar orbit; the degree to which these flags retain their original colors remains unknown.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143">
        <a href="#cite_note-143">
         [143]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In a November 16, 2009, editorial,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">
         The New York Times
        </a>
       </i>
       opined:
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        [T]here's something terribly wistful about these photographs of the Apollo landing sites. The detail is such that if Neil Armstrong were walking there now, we could make him out, make out his footsteps even, like the astronaut footpath clearly visible in the photos of the Apollo 14 site. Perhaps the wistfulness is caused by the sense of simple grandeur in those Apollo missions. Perhaps, too, it’s a reminder of the risk we all felt after the Eagle had landed – the possibility that it might be unable to lift off again and the astronauts would be stranded on the Moon. But it may also be that a photograph like this one is as close as we’re able to come to looking directly back into the human past...
       </p>
       <p>
        There the [Apollo 11] lunar module sits, parked just where it landed 40 years ago, as if it still really were 40 years ago and all the time since merely imaginary.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt_lro_lm_img_144-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-nyt_lro_lm_img-144">
          [144]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">
        Legacy
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Legacy">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Science_and_engineering">
        Science and engineering
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Science and engineering">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NASA_spin-off_technologies" title="NASA spin-off technologies">
        NASA spin-off technologies
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Apollo program has been called the greatest technological achievement in human history.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145">
        <a href="#cite_note-145">
         [145]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146">
        <a href="#cite_note-146">
         [146]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Apollo stimulated many areas of technology, leading to over 1,800 spinoff products as of 2015.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147">
        <a href="#cite_note-147">
         [147]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Guidance_Computer" title="Apollo Guidance Computer">
        flight computer
       </a>
       design used in both the Lunar and Command Modules was, along with the
       <a href="/wiki/UGM-27_Polaris" title="UGM-27 Polaris">
        Polaris
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/LGM-30_Minuteman" title="LGM-30 Minuteman">
        Minuteman
       </a>
       missile systems, the driving force behind early research into
       <a href="/wiki/Integrated_circuit" title="Integrated circuit">
        integrated circuits
       </a>
       (IC). By 1963, Apollo was using 60 percent of the United States' production of ICs. The crucial difference between the requirements of Apollo and the missile programs was Apollo's much greater need for reliability. While the Navy and Air Force could work around reliability problems by deploying more missiles, the political and financial cost of failure of an Apollo mission was unacceptably high.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMindell2008125-131_148-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMindell2008125-131-148">
         [148]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_impact">
        Cultural impact
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Cultural impact">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NASA-Apollo8-Dec24-Earthrise.jpg">
         <img alt="The Earth over the lunar horizon, photographed by the Apollo 8 crew" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2400" data-file-width="2400" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/NASA-Apollo8-Dec24-Earthrise.jpg/220px-NASA-Apollo8-Dec24-Earthrise.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/NASA-Apollo8-Dec24-Earthrise.jpg/330px-NASA-Apollo8-Dec24-Earthrise.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/NASA-Apollo8-Dec24-Earthrise.jpg/440px-NASA-Apollo8-Dec24-Earthrise.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NASA-Apollo8-Dec24-Earthrise.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Earthrise" title="Earthrise">
           Earthrise
          </a>
         </i>
         photograph taken by Apollo 8 on December 24, 1968. "Everything that I ever knew – my life, my loved ones, the Navy – everything, the whole world was behind my thumb." –
         <a href="/wiki/Jim_Lovell" title="Jim Lovell">
          James Lovell
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The crew of Apollo 8 sent the first live televised pictures of the Earth and the Moon back to Earth, and read from the creation story in the
       <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Genesis" title="Book of Genesis">
        Book of Genesis
       </a>
       , on Christmas Eve 1968.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149">
        <a href="#cite_note-149">
         [149]
        </a>
       </sup>
       An estimated one quarter of the population of the world saw—either live or delayed—the Christmas Eve transmission during the ninth orbit of the Moon,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150">
        <a href="#cite_note-150">
         [150]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and an estimated one fifth of the population of the world watched the live transmission of the Apollo 11 moonwalk.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151">
        <a href="#cite_note-151">
         [151]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3002" data-file-width="3000" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg/220px-The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg/330px-The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg/440px-The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/The_Blue_Marble" title="The Blue Marble">
           The Blue Marble
          </a>
         </i>
         photograph taken on December 7, 1972 during Apollo 17. "We went to explore the Moon, and in fact discovered the Earth." –
         <a href="/wiki/Eugene_Cernan" title="Eugene Cernan">
          Eugene Cernan
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Apollo program also affected
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_activism" title="Environmental activism">
        environmental activism
       </a>
       in the 1970s due to photos taken by the astronauts. The most famous, taken by the Apollo 17 astronauts, is
       <a href="/wiki/The_Blue_Marble" title="The Blue Marble">
        The Blue Marble
       </a>
       . This image, which was released during a surge in environmentalism, became a symbol of the environmental movement, as a depiction of Earth's frailty, vulnerability, and isolation amid the vast expanse of space.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Petsko_152-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Petsko-152">
         [152]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       According to
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Economist" title="The Economist">
         The Economist
        </a>
       </i>
       , Apollo succeeded in accomplishing President Kennedy's goal of taking on the Soviet Union in the
       <a href="/wiki/Space_Race" title="Space Race">
        Space Race
       </a>
       , and beat it by accomplishing a singular and significant achievement, and thereby showcased the superiority of the
       <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">
        free-market system
       </a>
       as represented by the US. The publication noted the irony that in order to achieve the goal, the program required the organization of tremendous public resources within a vast, centralized government bureaucracy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153">
        <a href="#cite_note-153">
         [153]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       There are those who, despite evidence to the contrary, deny that the Moon landings took place. The Apollo
       <a href="/wiki/Moon_landing_conspiracy_theories" title="Moon landing conspiracy theories">
        Moon landing hoax
       </a>
       claims helped propel
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_conspiracy_theories" title="List of conspiracy theories">
        conspiracy theories
       </a>
       into a quasi-political narrative.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154">
        <a href="#cite_note-154">
         [154]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Apollo_11_broadcast_data_restoration_project">
        Apollo 11 broadcast data restoration project
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Apollo 11 broadcast data restoration project">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_11_missing_tapes" title="Apollo 11 missing tapes">
        Apollo 11 missing tapes
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       As part of Apollo 11's 40th anniversary in 2009, NASA spearheaded an effort to digitally restore the existing videotapes of the mission's live televised moonwalk.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155">
        <a href="#cite_note-155">
         [155]
        </a>
       </sup>
       After an exhaustive three-year search for missing tapes of the original video of the Apollo 11 moonwalk, NASA concluded the data tapes had more than likely been accidentally erased.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NPR_tapes_156-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NPR_tapes-156">
         [156]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        We're all saddened that they're not there. We all wish we had 20-20 hindsight. I don't think anyone in the NASA organization did anything wrong, I think it slipped through the cracks, and nobody's happy about it.
       </p>
       <div class="templatequotecite">
        —
        <cite>
         Dick Nafzger, TV Specialist, NASA Goddard Space Flight Center
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NPR_tapes_156-1">
          <a href="#cite_note-NPR_tapes-156">
           [156]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </cite>
       </div>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       The Moon landing data was recorded by a special
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_TV_camera" title="Apollo TV camera">
        Apollo TV camera
       </a>
       which recorded in a format incompatible with broadcast TV. This resulted in lunar footage that had to be converted for the live television broadcast and stored on magnetic telemetry tapes. During the following years, a magnetic tape shortage prompted NASA to remove massive numbers of magnetic tapes from the
       <a href="/wiki/National_Archives_and_Records_Administration" title="National Archives and Records Administration">
        National Archives and Records Administration
       </a>
       to be recorded over with newer satellite data. Stan Lebar, who led the team that designed and built the lunar television camera at
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Westinghouse_Electric_(1886)" title="Westinghouse Electric (1886)">
        Westinghouse Electric Corporation
       </a>
       , also worked with Nafzger to try to locate the missing tapes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NPR_tapes_156-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-NPR_tapes-156">
         [156]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        So I don't believe that the tapes exist today at all. It was a hard thing to accept. But there was just an overwhelming amount of evidence that led us to believe that they just don't exist anymore. And you have to accept reality.
       </p>
       <div class="templatequotecite">
        —
        <cite>
         Stan Lebar, lunar television camera designer, Westinghouse Electric Corporation
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NPR_tapes_156-3">
          <a href="#cite_note-NPR_tapes-156">
           [156]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </cite>
       </div>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       With a budget of $230,000, the surviving original lunar broadcast data from Apollo 11 was compiled by Nafzger and assigned to
       <a href="/wiki/Lowry_Digital" title="Lowry Digital">
        Lowry Digital
       </a>
       for restoration. The video was processed to remove random noise and camera shake without destroying historical legitimacy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moon_footage_restore_157-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Moon_footage_restore-157">
         [157]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The images were from tapes in
       <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">
        Australia
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/CBS_News" title="CBS News">
        CBS News
       </a>
       archive, and
       <a href="/wiki/Kinescope" title="Kinescope">
        kinescope
       </a>
       recordings made at Johnson Space Center. The restored video, remaining in black and white, contains conservative digital enhancements and did not include sound quality improvements.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moon_footage_restore_157-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Moon_footage_restore-157">
         [157]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Depictions_on_film">
        Depictions on film
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Depictions on film">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Documentaries">
        Documentaries
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Documentaries">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Numerous documentary films cover the Apollo program and the Space Race, including:
      </p>
      <div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em;">
       <ul>
        <li>
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Moonwalk_One" title="Moonwalk One">
           Moonwalk One
          </a>
         </i>
         (1970)
        </li>
        <li>
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/For_All_Mankind" title="For All Mankind">
           For All Mankind
          </a>
         </i>
         (1989)
        </li>
        <li>
         "Moon" from the BBC miniseries
         <i>
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Planets_(TV_miniseries)" title="The Planets (TV miniseries)">

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Docudramas">
        Docudramas
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Docudramas">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The Apollo program, or certain missions, have been
       <a href="/wiki/Docudrama" title="Docudrama">
        dramatized
       </a>
       in
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Apollo_13_(film)" title="Apollo 13 (film)">
         Apollo 13
        </a>
       </i>
       (1995),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Apollo_11_(film)" title="Apollo 11 (film)">
         Apollo 11
        </a>
       </i>
       (1996),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/From_the_Earth_to_the_Moon_(miniseries)" title="From the Earth to the Moon (miniseries)">
         From the Earth to the Moon
        </a>
       </i>
       (1998),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Dish" title="The Dish">
         The Dish
        </a>
       </i>
       (2000),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Space_Race_(TV_series)" title="Space Race (TV series)">
         Space Race
        </a>
       </i>
       (2005), and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Moonshot_(film)" title="Moonshot (film)">
         Moonshot
        </a>
       </i>
       (2009).
      </p>

         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Chariots+for+Apollo%3A+The+Untold+Story+Behind+the+Race+to+the+Moon&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.pub=Avon+Books&amp;rft.date=1999&amp;rft_id=info%3Aoclcnum%2F41579174&amp;rft.isbn=0-380-80261-9&amp;rft.aulast=Pellegrino&amp;rft.aufirst=Charles+R.&amp;rft.au=Stoff%2C+Joshua&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AApollo+program">
